---
title: "Grub Doesn't Offer Non-low latency Kernel in Grub Menu (Studio Version 15.10)"
date: 2016-03-15
forum: Ubuntu Studio
---

### Post by LaneLester on 2016-03-15
I recently installed Studio 15.10, and I saw that "(lowlatency)" was part of the top item in the grub menu. I didn't know what that was, so I googled a bit and learned that it's best to use the default kernel unless the low latency is needed.

The second item in the grub menu is just "Ubuntu," which I assumed is the default kernel. So I set about editing /etc/default/grub to change the default boot to this second item.

However, when I ran update-grub, I noticed that there seems to be no difference between the first two grub options:
[INDENT]Generating grub configuration file ...
Found linux image: /boot/**vmlinuz-4.2.0-30-lowlatency**
Found initrd image: /boot/initrd.img-4.2.0-30-lowlatency
Found linux image: /boot/**vmlinuz-4.2.0-30-lowlatency**
Found initrd image: /boot/initrd.img-4.2.0-30-lowlatency
Found linux image: /boot/vmlinuz-4.2.0-16-lowlatency
Found initrd image: /boot/initrd.img-4.2.0-16-lowlatency
Found memtest86+ image: /boot/memtest86+.elf
Found memtest86+ image: /boot/memtest86+.bin
[/INDENT]

So is there no way to run a non-low latency kernel? If not, does it matter?

Lane

---

### Post by 1clue on 2016-03-15
Low latency kernels use a preemptive task manager. Normal kernels are somewhat less aggressive in their preemption, and server kernels are non-preemtive. I understand items 1 and 3 but never got around to figuring out 2.

What you want on your system depends on what sort of things you use it for. Gaming systems, among others, want instant response to user input so more cpu time is dedicated to that.

The thing is that context switching on a preemptive kernel takes cpu time. So a low-latency kernel has lower all-around throughput. A non-preemtive (server) kernel has highest throughput.

---

### Post by LaneLester on 2016-03-15
Thanks, 1clue! You've provided more evidence why I should NOT be running a low latency kernel. But as I said, grub-update seemed to only find a low latency kernel for Studio.

Lane

---

### Post by 1clue on 2016-03-15
OK well chances are if your computer is able to easily handle your use case, then it won't matter one way or the other unless you need a low-latency kernel.  You'll see slightly higher cpu utilization but that's it, you won't really notice unless you  hit the bumper, so to speak.

So go to synaptic and search on 'kernel'.  You can see below I have a generic kernel, and yours will show lowlatency.

Frankly I've never messed with the kernel on Ubuntu, so I'm not positive on this part.  Generally if I need to mess with the kernel I'm running Gentoo.

At any rate, I think that you should be able to simply install the generic kernel and then boot from it.  I would suggest trying both simultaneously at first and manually choose the generic one.

I would advise you have a bootable disk/cdrom/usb stick handy in case you mess something up, that will let you get everything back.

### Post by QDR06VV9 on 2016-03-15
From the author of the blogpost linked by a ubuntu fan: [http://sevencapitalsins.wordpress.com/2007/08/10/low-latency-kernel-wtf/](http://sevencapitalsins.wordpress.com/2007/08/10/low-latency-kernel-wtf/)

That blog post doesn't present any fact, it's only theory. It's the way it works, actually: the processor "stops" more frequently to see if there are some processes requiring immediate attention. That means that those processes will be executed before the others, so you won't skip frames when encoding, or have huge delay times between mouse clicks and enemy deaths. It doesn't mean that all processes will end sooner: actually the CPU is losing a bigger portion of its time deciding what process will be executed next, and doing the context switch. So the total execution time is longer, and that's why no one runs a preemptible kernel on webserver or database machines. But a preemptible 300Hz (or even 1000Hz) kernel is the best for gameservers.

But nowadays processors have many cores, so when there are few processes requiring attention they can easily be allocated on a different core rather than waiting for a core to take it.

(stackexchange requires me references/personal experience: I'm an electronic engineer, bloodthirsty noobgamer mantaining several gameservers at [http://www.gamezoo.it](http://www.gamezoo.it) ).

So, as a rule of thumb, I'd say: if your processor is a powerful number-crunching high-frequency quad-core AND you don't usually open tons of webpages while encoding/decoding/gaming (huh), you could just try the generic (or i686, or amd64 if they exist) kernel and have the highest possible throughput (i.e., the raw number-crunching the processor is able to do). If you experience problems (they should really be minor) or your machine is slightly less powerful than the top of the market, go for the -preempt.

If you're on a low-end machine that has only one or two cores, then try the -lowlatency. You could also try the -realtime, but you'll find that it tends to block processes until the "real-time" ones have finished their job. I believe the realtime kernel isn't the "vanilla" one, but has the CONFIG_PREEMPT_RT patch applied. I think that realtime kernels are only for those who have to build a single application on embedded systems, so usual desktop users shouldn't have real benefits because they usually run a fair number of applications at the same time.

Finally, the most relevant kernel options if you want to recompile your kernel yourself to have a low-latency desktop are:

```
PREEMPT=y

and:

CONFIG_1000_HZ=y

To add some powersaving you may check this one:

CONFIG_NO_HZ=y
```
I run Low latency kernels almost exclusively..
Hope this was helpfull

---

### Post by 1clue on 2016-03-15
Of course, the difference between preemptive and low-latency will be the rate of context switches.  I don't know why I didn't think of that.

Personally on my Gentoo boxes I choose non-preemptive kernels. My hardware all has at least 4 real cores and the normal task scheduler does well enough that I never see a hang.  But that's all server hardware.

As I pointed out above my Ubuntu workstation is using the generic kernel.  Don't see any problems.

### Post by 1clue on 2016-03-15
OK so we need to take care of that before you remove low-latency kernels.

I'm not sure how to do this. There has to be some sort of driver you're missing.  Usually I would see what's not working, figure out what the driver is supposed to be and then recompile the kernel with that module turned on.  I'm pretty old-school like that.

With Ubuntu, it seems you're not supposed to have to compile, I figured the kernel would be "everything on" configuration.  Surely for keyboards and mice.

Do you have a second computer, preferably with ssh?  See if you can ssh into the frozen box.  If it's just input devices that are broken then you might fix that from remote.

How familiar are you with Linux?  Do you know what modules are?

### Post by LaneLester on 2016-03-16
Well, there's one more chapter in this saga. I went back to synaptic and searched for "linux." I discovered that I had failed to install all of the needed **generic** packages. I had assumed that, like other packages, selecting one would pick up all that were needed.

I'm running **generic** now, and all seems to be well. Now to fiddle with** /etc/default/grub** and see if I can figure out which number to use to get the generic to boot as the default, rather than the low-latency that update-grub sets as the default.

**Later**: After a few failed attempts to set **generic** as the default choice, I found [this page]("https://help.ubuntu.com/community/Grub2/Submenus") that told me how to do it. Because the **generic** option is in a submenu, the correct line is **GRUB_DEFAULT="2>3"** because the Advanced Options submenu is the third item in the main menu, and **generic** is the fourth option in the submenu.

If I let grub time out, it boots the **generic**. To select it immediately takes two **Enter**s, one to get in the Advanced Options and another to choose the **generic**.

Lane
